    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2392991812" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2392991812" />
    <a title="Josiah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah"><img alt="Josiah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239682454p2/1914201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2392991812?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josiah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josiah" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah">Josiah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781481449663" href="/book/show/30312819-at-the-edge-of-the-universe">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2392991812" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14833738974199592066">Released a year after <i>We Are the Ants</i>, which came out in 2016, <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i> reinforces some of the recurring style and themes of Shaun David Hutchinson's YA literature. Repressed trauma, same-sex romance, unusually intelligent and articulate characters, and speculative science fiction are concepts the author loves to engage with, and he doubles down on that in this novel. Seventeen-year-old Ozzie Pinkerton is a high school senior in his small hometown of Cloud Lake, Florida. The l</span>
  <span id="freeText14833738974199592066" style="display:none">Released a year after <i>We Are the Ants</i>, which came out in 2016, <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i> reinforces some of the recurring style and themes of Shaun David Hutchinson's YA literature. Repressed trauma, same-sex romance, unusually intelligent and articulate characters, and speculative science fiction are concepts the author loves to engage with, and he doubles down on that in this novel. Seventeen-year-old Ozzie Pinkerton is a high school senior in his small hometown of Cloud Lake, Florida. The lens through which Ozzie views reality recently suffered a serious distortion when Tommy Ross, his boyfriend since middle school, vanished from existence. Ozzie's parents and brother have no recollection of Tommy. Neither does Mrs. Ross, or the kids at school, including Ozzie's best friends Lua and Dustin. Even Ozzie's journal entries, which date back many years, have changed to omit Tommy, reality reshaping in his absence to account for facts that would otherwise be inexplicable. Ozzie comes up with radical theories to explain Tommy's disappearance: we live in a multiverse; reality is actually a virtual reality created by scientists; the true nature of existence is nonlinear, vastly different than humans perceive. Ozzie clings to his memories of Tommy in hope he'll return someday as abruptly as he was taken away. He and Tommy loved each other too much for it to end without recourse.<br /><br /> Senior year has crazy surprises in store. Lua's band is heating up the local scene, expanding their popularity so that national success seems an attainable goal. Lua is too talented to be stuck forever in Cloud Lake; the world deserves to fawn over her. It's at a performance by her music group, Your Mom's a Paradox, that Ozzie gets to talking with Calvin, a boy from school he didn't know until recently. Last year Calvin earned impressive grades while also dominating on the wrestling team, but he's been a shell of himself this year. He quit wrestling and his grades have plummeted, which is why the teacher assigns him to work with Ozzie on a year-end project designing a roller coaster that could function in reality. Ozzie's smart, but Calvin used to be smarter, and their roller coaster could be phenomenal if Calvin is willing to engage. It looks like a lost cause until Ozzie runs into Calvin at the club where Lua performs. A club for same-sex dating. Ozzie wants to hold out for Tommy's improbable return from oblivion, but Calvin is here now, and seems to like Ozzie. Should he continue keeping Calvin at arm's length?<br /><br /> "You can choose to be happy with what life gives you...or spend your life miserable. I choose happiness. It's really that simple."<br /><br /> —Dustin, <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i>, P. 218<br /><br /> So much of what Ozzie loved about Tommy, he also sees in Calvin. The boy's comprehension of science far outpaces Ozzie's. Calvin designs a roller coaster that would be a spectacular attraction at any amusement park if the coaster were real. He even understands Ozzie's reluctance to turn his back on Tommy, whom everyone else impatiently insists is a figment of Ozzie's hyperactive imagination. Ozzie doesn't know what he'd do if Tommy suddenly emerged from the ether to find him cozying up to Calvin, but he's not sure he can keep his life on pause to wait for his missing boyfriend. What's more, Calvin confides in Ozzie about the grim secret that turned him from a model student-athlete into an angsty pariah, on the condition that Ozzie keep the revelation to himself. Ozzie hesitates to agree, but he messed up a similar situation with Tommy, and doesn't want to make another big mistake now. He has to hope Calvin can handle his own disturbing history.<br /><br /> Tommy's vanishing act isn't the only bizarre phenomenon Ozzie has noticed. The universe is shrinking. From fifteen billion light-years across on the first page of <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i>, Ozzie regularly notes how much smaller it's becoming. Students, teachers, and textbooks all know the universe to be smaller than Ozzie remembers. Fifteen billion light-years? No, the universe is only <i>fourteen</i> billion light-years across. Then twelve, nine, one, and then it's measured in astronomical units rather than light-years. Eventually the Milky Way is the only galaxy; later, our solar system is alone in the universe. The Sun pops out of existence, and only Ozzie remembers that big ball of burning gas in the sky. The tragedy of our shrinking universe is important, but Ozzie has other concerns. Hoping for a future with Tommy seems impractical, but is he willing to sacrifice that hope for Calvin? Ozzie's feelings for Calvin run strong, but the boy's troubles are a dark void sucking Ozzie in like an astronomical black hole. Their relationship could bring both of them to destruction if Ozzie doesn't figure out his priorities with Calvin and decide to act on them, whatever it costs them both.<br /><br /> If <i>We Are the Ants</i> was a strange book, <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i> is stranger still, but clearly a product of Shaun David Hutchinson's biting intellect. The story ranges from beautiful, insightful passages to tongue-in-cheek <i>We Are the Ants</i> references; from distressing personal dilemmas to intimate encounters that quicken the heart and mind. There are bits like the following, a description of the scent of books which will resonate with book lovers: "It's more than the smell of paper; it's the smell of the high seas and adventure and far-off worlds. It's the smell of a billion billion words, each a portal to somewhere new." I've felt that every time I explored a library or bookshop. Shaun David Hutchinson's fans should recognize the brief crossover with <i>We Are the Ants</i> on page one hundred one, and it's not the only nod to that book in <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i>.<br /><br /> A seminal moment comes when Calvin asks Ozzie how it feels to be in love. Calvin isn't sure he's ever experienced it, but Ozzie isn't shy about reminding people that he and Tommy loved each other, and Calvin is curious. Ozzie's wise answer affirms that his relationship with Tommy couldn't be mere fantasy. "We've all got secrets, you know? We've all got things about ourselves we hate and these dark places inside of us we're terrified to show people. We live in constant fear that someone is going to discover the rotting corpses we keep buried in those dark places, and that when they do, they'll despise us for them...Being in love with someone is knowing that no matter what you show them, no matter what you've done, they'll never reject you." Only someone who loves you could peer inside and not instinctually judge the ugliness that is part of your soul. One with no emotional attachment would recoil in revulsion and denounce you before fleeing and never looking back, but love binds hearts together. It provides reason not to leave despite our congenital and acquired failings. Is it any wonder, then, that Ozzie desires to remain loyal to Tommy even if all hope has departed?<br /><br /> "It's impossible to let go of the people we love. Pieces of them remain embedded inside of us like shrapnel. Every breath causes those fragments to burrow through our muscles, nearer to our hearts. And we think the pain will kill us, but it won't. Eventually, scar tissue forms around those twisted splinters like cocoons. They remain part of us, but slowly hurt less."<br /><br /> —<i>At the Edge of the Universe</i>, P. 129<br /><br /> Ozzie's high school graduation is at hand, and having to make decisions for himself in the real world is as frightening in its own way as the universe collapsing on itself. How should Ozzie know whether leaving Cloud Lake to attend college or staying home is his best bet? But he can't rely on his parents to chart his course forever. Opportunities open up that will be accessible only briefly, and Ozzie agonizes over each one. As he reflects early in the book, "Life's truest horror is a door that slams shut that can never be opened again." It takes close to four hundred pages before Ozzie rethinks that. It's daunting to think of bypassing golden opportunities, but perhaps it's an unavoidable part of life. "Maybe some doors that slam shut behind you and can't ever be opened again aren't the scariest things in the world after all. Maybe some doors are better off closed. That way we can focus on the ones still open in front of us." It's natural to feel remorse over roads not taken that could have made all the difference, but there are open doors ahead and some that have yet to even open, and you can't evaluate them if you're busy dwelling on past regrets. You may find a door where you'll be welcomed into the environment your heart longs for. "Life is life. It happens, it goes on. Eventually, it ends, but other lives continue, new ones begin. That's just the way of it. My life would keep going on until the day it didn't, and I could either make the best of it or waste it wishing for what I didn't and might never have." Don't lose out on the beauty available to you today; figure out what you want from life and how to go about getting it. The rewards of peace and love may fall your way when you least expect.<br /><br /> "The only thing in life that's forever is death. You can change your mind about everything else."<br /><br /> —Mrs. Ross, <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i>, P. 248<br /><br /> This isn't an easy book to write a synopsis of, but I tried to follow Tommy's guideline for essays: "Tell 'em you're gonna tell 'em. Tell 'em. Tell 'em you told 'em." <i>We Are the Ants</i> is intellectually deeper and significantly more emotional, but <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i> is good, and I'm rounding my two-and-a-half-star rating up to three. There are extremely complex questions in these pages, not only about moral decisions, but elements of the plot. Was the universe actually shrinking? Did Tommy truly vanish, somehow leaving Ozzie as the only person who remembers him? The plot is an intricate work of art we can't entirely grasp, and the lingering questions leave more confusion than in <i>We Are the Ants</i>. Regardless, I'll never regret reading this, and I recommend <i>At the Edge of the Universe</i> for anyone struggling to define their life. Ozzie's story is a comfort for its reminder that in your most severe moments of existential uncertainty, you're not the only one feeling that way. The universe can be a lonely place, but it's big enough to hold anything you dream. All you have to do is get started.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14833738974199592066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1886558198" />
    <a title="ti ✧" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36518278-ti"><img alt="ti ✧" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550278190p2/36518278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1886558198?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ti ✧" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ti ✧" href="/user/show/36518278-ti">ti ✧</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1886558198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5049419985754371096"><i>this will be a very rambling review because i can't think clearly when i think about this book.</i><br/><br/>last year in january i read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23677341.We_Are_the_Ants' title='We Are the Ants by Shaun David Hutchinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>We Are the Ants</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3130410.Shaun_David_Hutchinson' title='Shaun David Hutchinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shaun David Hutchinson</a> and i thought it was one of the most amazing books i ever read. when i heard he was publishing another kind of sci-fi realistic novel i was screaming with joy.<br/><br/>i have to say i was very surprised to find myself loving this even more than his other <i>perfect</i> novels.<br/><br/>first of all, i love the <b>idea</b> of the book: the universe is shrinking and p</span>
  <span id="freeText5049419985754371096" style="display:none"><i>this will be a very rambling review because i can't think clearly when i think about this book.</i><br /><br />last year in january i read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23677341.We_Are_the_Ants" title="We Are the Ants by Shaun David Hutchinson" rel="nofollow noopener">We Are the Ants</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3130410.Shaun_David_Hutchinson" title="Shaun David Hutchinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Shaun David Hutchinson</a> and i thought it was one of the most amazing books i ever read. when i heard he was publishing another kind of sci-fi realistic novel i was screaming with joy.<br /><br />i have to say i was very surprised to find myself loving this even more than his other <i>perfect</i> novels.<br /><br />first of all, i love the <b>idea</b> of the book: the universe is shrinking and people are disappearing and forgotten, memories are being rewritten and only one boy can see it. shaun is able to make a very unrealistic thing seem very, well, real. <br /><br />the <b>diversity</b> in this book is a dream. straight, gay, asexual, bisexual, gender-fluid, white, poc ... and it doesn't feel forced whatsoever. <br /><br />there are also really <b>difficult topics</b> in this book which shaun writes about so respectfully and with such great understanding, e.g. self-harm, suicidal thoughts, sexual assault, rape, drug abuse, domestic abuse and a lot more. <br /><br />and of course <b>the characters</b>. ozzie, tommy, lua, dustin and calvin are such complex and deep characters with so many sides to them that they not even for one second feel fictional. they feel like real people with real flaws and moods and fears and real lives. they change their minds and make it hard for you to understand their motives sometimes but that’s what makes them so great. <br />i personally loved calvin the most because i could relate to him and i just loved him so much. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[his indifference to his own happiness and him cutting himself to forget the emotional pain and yet him being overly enthusiatic and caring about other things <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is something i experience every day. <br /><br />finally, <b>the theories</b> of why the universe appears to be shrinking is one of my favourite things in this book. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">["what if we're in a computer simulation?" <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> i would have to lie if i said i hadn't thought about all this for hours on end.<br /><br />i just can't recommend this book enough. it's genuinely <i>perfect and breathtaking</i>.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1644153891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1644153891" />
    <a title="Eliza Rapsodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6561375-eliza-rapsodia"><img alt="Eliza Rapsodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597687917p2/6561375.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1644153891?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eliza Rapsodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eliza Rapsodia" href="/user/show/6561375-eliza-rapsodia">Eliza Rapsodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781481449663" href="/book/show/30312819-at-the-edge-of-the-universe">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Todo el mundo / Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Eliza by: </span>
      Nadie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6561375-eliza-rapsodia?shelf=on-my-shelf">on-my-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6561375-eliza-rapsodia?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6561375-eliza-rapsodia?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6561375-eliza-rapsodia?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1644153891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16538270940495874817"><b>REVIEW IN ENGLISH </b><br/><br/><b>It is a truth universally acknowledged that I highly recommend Shaun David Hutchinson to any living reader form. </b> This is my third novel of his that I have read and I can say I will NEVER get off of this train. I love his books so much and this was not the exception.<br/><br/>Oswald Pinkerton, Ozzie for friends, is a young boy who is in his last year of school and works part time in a bookstore (I want that job, damn). His life in the small town of Cloud Lake could not be more boring, on</span>
  <span id="freeText16538270940495874817" style="display:none"><b>REVIEW IN ENGLISH </b><br><br><b>It is a truth universally acknowledged that I highly recommend Shaun David Hutchinson to any living reader form. </b> This is my third novel of his that I have read and I can say I will NEVER get off of this train. I love his books so much and this was not the exception.<br><br>Oswald Pinkerton, Ozzie for friends, is a young boy who is in his last year of school and works part time in a bookstore (I want that job, damn). His life in the small town of Cloud Lake could not be more boring, only his best friends Lua and Dustin and his boyfriend and friend since childhood Tommy are what matters. But things go wrong one day. <b>Tommy suddenly dissapears. The strangest thing is that Ozzie seems to be the only one who remembers him, nobody else. </b> He has this feeling that the universe is contracting and that's why Tommy's mother does not even remember her own son. <b>Ozzy is ready to find him before the universe swallow them whole.</b><br><br>After reading We are the ants, <b>the premise of this book may sound similar: a supernatural circumstance about something strange that is happening and the main character knows about this and must find a way to solve it.</b> It's a science fiction premise that Shaun knows how to execute well, but it's not just about that. The story is also about love, destiny and self-discovery <b>mixed up with a whole buch of situations and issues that are more common than they seem: divorce, financial crisis, sexual abuse, domestic abuse, poverty and the question of sexual and gender identity.</b><br><br>Ozzie is the protagonist and narrator. Through him we meet all the characters: Lua, a fluid gender girl-guy who is very talented with music; Dustin, a young Asian asexual (confirmed by the author) who goes through a difficult situation at home and Calvin, a smart and athletic boy but is going through a problematic phase for some time and attracts Ozzie's attention. <b>Each secondary character is fleshed out and well developed; even through flashbacks we get to know Tommy, Ozzie's boyfriend and why Ozzie loved him so much.</b><br><br><b>Shaun's writing is amazing, it makes the story real, close and it creates exciting, hard and also very beautiful moments.</b> Ozzie's journey is not only to understand that he is not crazy, that he does not have to go to therapy because his parents force him but because talking about his problems can help him to express everything he feels<b> and because he is going through a moment in his life in which you have to make decisions and he must learn to face them.</b><br><br>Despite its huge positive points, there are details that did not convince me that much. <b>There were times when the story was slow and difficult to went through and the final resolution, although it is creative and imaginative, it made noise behind my ear. </b> That's why I think that having a premise like that can go very bad or very well. In this case it's good, but with its little details.<br><br>This is a novel that involved me emotionally and made me empathize with the characters and their situation.<b> It is a beautiful and hard story about moving forward, looking for your future and learning to make decisions.</b> It is a real pity that this author has not been translated into Spanish and more people can read his books. <br><br><b>I have to remark the author's note at the end. He's encouraging people that, like him, have gone through depression and suicidal thoughts to go and seek help. That it's OK, and you don't have to face everything alone, that you can keep going.</b><br><br><b>**************************<br>RESEÑA EN ESPAÑOL</b><br><br>En este blog se sabe 
  <b>que recomiendo con mucho entusiasmo todos los libros de este autor de Florida</b>
 y esta es la tercera novela suya que he leído. Si quieren saber más en detalle lo que opino de sus otros dos libros pueden visitar mis reseñas <a href="https://rapsodia-literaria.blogspot.com/2016/01/the-five-stages-of-andrew-brawley-de.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">aquí</a> y <a href="https://rapsodia-literaria.blogspot.com/2017/03/we-are-ants-de-shaun-david-hutchinson.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">aquí</a>.<br><br>Oswald Pinkerton, Ozzie para los amigos, es un jovencito que está en su último año de colegio y trabaja medio tiempo en una librería. Su vida en el pequeño pueblo de Cloud Lake no podría ser más aburrida y simplona, <b>
  solo lo arreglan sus mejores amigos Lua y Dustin y su novio y amigo desde la infancia llamado Tommy.
</b> Pero las cosas se ponen mal cuando un día, Tommy desaparece de repente. Lo más extraño es que Ozzie parece ser el único que lo recuerda, nadie más. El tiene el presentimiento que el universo se está contrayendo cada día y por eso ni la madre de Tommy recuerda a su hijo. <b>
  Y por eso Ozzie está dispuesto a buscarlo.
</b><br><br><img height="213" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537632805i/26338068.jpg" width="320" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>
  Fotografía de Taryn Manning en Unsplash. Crédito
   <a href="https://unsplash.com/photos/7Fxkv4MFQqI" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">aquí</a>
<br><br>Después de leer We are the ants, la premisa de este libro puede sonar parecida: una circunstancia sobrenatural
  <b> sobre algo extraño que está sucediendo en el universo y el protagonista sabe de esto y debe buscar la forma de solucionarlo. </b>
Se trata de una pregunta de ciencia ficción que creo que Shaun sabe ejecutar bien, pero no se trata solo de eso. La historia es mucho más que la búsqueda de la persona amada y de uno mismo, sino todo un conjunto de situaciones y temas que son más comunes de lo que parecen:<b> divorcio, crisis financiera, abuso sexual, maltrato intrafamiliar, pobreza y la cuestión del descubrimiento sexual y de género.</b><br><br>Ozzie es el protagonista y narrador de la historia. A través de él conoceremos a todos los personajes: su mejor amiga Lua, un chico género fluido que es muy talentosa con la música y puede llegar muy lejos; Dustin, un joven asiático que pasa por una situación difícil en su casa <b>
  y Calvin, es un muchacho inteligente y atlético pero está pasando una fase problemática desde hace tiempo y atrae la atención de Ozzie
</b>. Podemos ver la situación familiar de cada uno porque en este libro los padres aparecen y hacen parte de la historia. Creo que cada personaje secundario está bien trabajado, incluso a través de flashbacks vamos conociendo a Tommy,<b> el novio de Ozzie y como fue su relación.</b><br><br><img height="163" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1537632805ra/26338069.gif" width="320" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>La escritura de Shaun hace que la historia se torne cercana y amena y que tenga frases y momentos emocionantes, duros y también muy bellos. El viaje de Ozzie no es solo para entender que no está loco, que no va a terapia porque sus padres lo obliguen<b>
   sino porque hablar de sus problemas puede ayudarle 
</b>a expresar todo lo que siente
  <b> y porque está atravesando un momento de su vida en el que hay que tomar decisiones y debe aprender a enfrentarlas.</b>
<br><br>A pesar de sus enormes puntos positivos, <b>
  hay detalles que no me han convencido tanto. 
</b>Hubo momentos en que la historia le costaba continuar y atravesaba baches narrativos y la resolución final del conflicto si bien es creativa e imaginativa, me hizo un poco de ruido detrás de la oreja. Por eso creo que contar con una premisa como las que usa el autor pueden salir muy mal o muy bien. En<b>
   este caso ha salido bien, pero con sus detallitos.
</b><br><br>Igualmente, ha sido una novela que me ha implicado emocionalmente y me ha hecho empatizar con los personajes y sus historias. <b>
  Es una bellísima y dura historia sobre seguir adelante, buscar tu futuro y aprender a tomar decisiones.
</b> Es una verdadera pena que este autor no haya sido traducido al español y más personas puedan conocer sus libros. <b>
  Y sobre todo la importantísima nota que el autor pone al final hablando y animando a otros como él que han pasado por situaciones de depresión y pensamientos suicidas que ESTÁ BIEN buscar ayuda
</b> y que los problemas no hay que enfrentarlos siempre solo. Que se puede seguir adelante. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16538270940495874817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2464824458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2464824458" />
    <a title="Iulia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50950278-iulia"><img alt="Iulia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523874313p2/50950278.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2464824458?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iulia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iulia" href="/user/show/50950278-iulia">Iulia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50950278-iulia?shelf=honorable-mentions">honorable-mentions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50950278-iulia?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2464824458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6446136238661160095">You know that feeling when you read a book that's so great you immediately set out on a quest to find another one like it? I read <i> We Are the Ants </i> by the same author and I absolutely loved it; it's actually one of my <i> super-favorites </i>. I picked up <i> At the Edge of the Universe </i> hoping that it would evoke in me a similar emotion, and I wasn't wrong; it absolutely delivered! Much like its predecessor, this is very much a character driven book, with beautifully constructed relationships and great c</span>
  <span id="freeText6446136238661160095" style="display:none">You know that feeling when you read a book that's so great you immediately set out on a quest to find another one like it? I read <i> We Are the Ants </i> by the same author and I absolutely loved it; it's actually one of my <i> super-favorites </i>. I picked up <i> At the Edge of the Universe </i> hoping that it would evoke in me a similar emotion, and I wasn't wrong; it absolutely delivered! Much like its predecessor, this is very much a character driven book, with beautifully constructed relationships and great character development. The whole "universe shrinking" gimmick (I use this word with its best connotation) kept me glued to the screen of my Kindle, counting the light-years and astronomical units along with Ozzie, our protagonist. While this is YA, and it sometimes reads as YA with lots of teen angst and internal monologues, that doesn't mean it lacks substance, Quite on the contrary, Shaun David Hutchinson doesn't shy away from heavy themes. Every single character, even the supporting ones, has a down-to-earth, visceral realness about them. The things they go through are at times heart-wrenching, and often there's no magical plot device which brings them back to safety so we, the readers, can be spared the sadness of it all. This is a book that's not afraid of loss, pain and illness; it doesn't show you the world through rose-colored glasses. Definitely worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6446136238661160095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1727006999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14438525214543678257">4.5/5 - I'll add Shaun David Hutchinson to my list of favourite authors, I guess.<br/>He always seems to write such powerful stories:<br/><b>You laugh. You cry. You have no idea what's going on or what's going to happen, but you stay tuned all the way because he has a way of writing such beautiful, realisticly flawed but understandable characters living in worlds that are so cleverly created.</b><br/><br/>And this is exactly what this book did. I laughed and I cried and I was confused but I loved it.<br/>I loved the little mom</span>
  <span id="freeText14438525214543678257" style="display:none">4.5/5 - I'll add Shaun David Hutchinson to my list of favourite authors, I guess.<br />He always seems to write such powerful stories:<br /><b>You laugh. You cry. You have no idea what's going on or what's going to happen, but you stay tuned all the way because he has a way of writing such beautiful, realisticly flawed but understandable characters living in worlds that are so cleverly created.</b><br /><br />And this is exactly what this book did. I laughed and I cried and I was confused but I loved it.<br />I loved the little moments about physics (yeah, I miss not learning about physics...) and even philosophy. As expected there were beautiful metaphors about struggling to find yourself, drowning or breathing under water, moving forward and closing doors...<br /><b>There was a whole bunch of diverse characters</b> (the queer youth represented in here is great - gender-fluid, gay, bi and even asexual - thank you!). <b>And a whole bunch of important issues</b> too. Mental health, hurting yourself, sexual abuse, domestic violence, financial trouble and more.<br /><br />Sometimes I thought there were a little TOO many important issues in this one book - maybe some could have been saved for a different story? <br />It seemed like really shitty things had to happen to every single character, which I don't think was necessary.<br />Especially as Ozzie, the main character, was going through all the issues over and over in his head so many times, sometimes I just wanted to say 'Okay, we know by now that this this and this is going on, move onto things we don't know'...<br />But in the end you can't complain for having an array of different people with a wide-range of problems.<br /><br />I also read the author's note at the end about his own struggles with mental health, depression, cutting and suicide attempts, he urges people to ask for help and how his books show people dealing with mental health in ways that aren't always the best. They are realistic, but you should learn that they are not always the best ways to deal with stuff. <br />It was very moving.<br /><br />So basically, I really enjoyed it.<br />I'm going to compare it to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1683607415?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">We Are The Ants</a> because that was one of my favourite books of 2016, the reason I picked this one up and had such high expectations. And to be honest it wasn't as good as WATA. But by no means was it bad.<br />It was great and well worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14438525214543678257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1961353067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1961353067" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9104633-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518977342p2/9104633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1961353067?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/9104633-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9104633-jason?shelf=0-2017-c-r-def">0-2017-c-r-def</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9104633-jason?shelf=00-lgbt">00-lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1961353067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4691423296152427887">up a notch to 2 because the lower ranking of recent books in the same genre has altered my perspective on this one <br/><br/>Until At the Edge of the Universe I was still debating if Shaun David Hutchinson was my guy. With The Five Stages of Andrew Brawley, I felt that the plot wasn't slammed down onto paper in a way that could max its potential. The idea of a kid in a hospital, with the staff unaware of a gent wandering around, seemed unrealistic, as did the way he connected with his environment and with</span>
  <span id="freeText4691423296152427887" style="display:none">up a notch to 2 because the lower ranking of recent books in the same genre has altered my perspective on this one <br /><br />Until At the Edge of the Universe I was still debating if Shaun David Hutchinson was my guy. With The Five Stages of Andrew Brawley, I felt that the plot wasn't slammed down onto paper in a way that could max its potential. The idea of a kid in a hospital, with the staff unaware of a gent wandering around, seemed unrealistic, as did the way he connected with his environment and with events. Developmentally it just didnt seem to add up. <br /><br />I moved onto Ants with grand hopes. I thought that surely, his writing would mature. Yet, while he provided a theme that was unusual, at least for young adult books, it fizzled as the pages folded and at the end was a massive disappointment.<br /><br />But I immediately picked up this novel. End of the world dystopia books have increased in numbers, from the sun vanishing, to comets heading for impact. Yet, the shrinking of the world, with references to Ants, and double that with a gay protagonist?!… boom.<br /><br />Yet, it has virtually the same themes. We have this obsession with space and scientific theories, divorce, depression, and unnecessary, unresolved trauma. Single parents. lower SES. Oh, and let’s not forget sexual assault. <br /><br />My problem with sexual assault in novels, particularly in Y/A, is that it is often taken on by authors as a way of creating internal chaos. It is a way to spin out changes in character behavior, such as anxiety and depression. And it’s easy, because sexual assault is almost always coupled with some shift in ‘normal’ behavior. It’s fucking lazy. J.H Trumble created one of the more successful portrayals of trauma and PTSD. This is Shaun’s second attempt with the theme of trauma, and it was an utter failure. There are wonderful resources out there that show the full capacity of the ways sexual assault and trauma typically crush a person, and I wish he’d make an attempt to understand these topics before he writes about them. Being blind of a topic’s intricacies is a travesty and a disservice to those challenged by them. Here’s the problem though, he isn’t, blind to them, as he speaks at the end about his own struggles with mental illness. So why then are they not submerged into his writing? Why doesn’t this story carry more substance, a deeper understanding, a rawness that other books fail to achieve? <br /><br /><br />While this, again, gave me a giant sigh, that wasn’t my biggest concern. Here's the central problem with the plot; the amount of absurdity. Going into this novel <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ it's understood that the world isn’t shrinking, but that Ozzie has crept into delusion. Here's the issue with that. Unless the world is really shrinking, or it's some sort of X-files shit, his world is cemented over a reality that continues while he is having a breakdown. Problem is, his delusion co-existed with the true reality. So, there needed to be an intersection between these two worlds (there was, in some cases… sorta), where they functioned as a piece of mesh. So, for instance, the 'real world' was supporting this delusion, such as with Shutter Island. This didnt happen, however, and as Ozzie continued to navigate this false reality, the real one continued at his feet. Here's where it could’ve worked. The scene where Ozzie's world has shrunk to just him, and his back is to the door, with something pushing on it, it was here things could have really gelled. A nice resolution would have been that the people functioning in the real world were following along with his delusions, and that they found Ozzie too stricken by his fictitious world, and decided to intervene. Ok, too shutter island. What if, then, Ozzie had vanished, and that his fake reality actually occurred in a location where this unhealthy state of mind was able to function without intervention from his friends/teachers/etc? He returned to his town, and on the other side of that door he'd find his friends and his parents? This isn’t what happened, and unless the exhaustion of staying up until 4:30 has created MY own false reality, his disillusions were just superimposed onto reality. There was so much potential to take on one of those theories that you made us suffer through, Shaun, but as we neared the end we realized it was just your preoccupation with science. These theories were everywhere, and it became clear that they were just a device to confuse us, and as Calvin dove into the abyss, it became self-evident that the only option left was a false reality. It would have been REALLYYYY effective to have it unfold as a real suicide, rather than a metaphor that suited the false reality; rather than a way to push Ozzie closer to his true sense of self. But, because Ozzie was caught in his own world— Lua always said he was self-centered—he accepted this suicide as just an exercise for Calvin to exorcise his personal grief. Bates motel anyone?! RAMBLING OF ANNOYANCES DONE. . After reading it, I was left with the image of a writer’s kid being tasked to complete an episode of the X-files.<br /><br />Characters... oh the diversity.... oh how splendid and shiny new to a genre that almost always focuses on white, gay men. <br /><br />Ozzie, a bright, handsome, and mature fellow, is living in a foreign place, one where his BF Tommy doesn't exist. From the start, almost in the first chapter, we figure out that he’s living in a parallel—Shaun’s endless throw down of theories makes this obvious—universe, but why, how, and what is in the future for Ozzie once he discovers these answers, is a mystery.<br /><br />Then there is Calvin. He's tossed into Ozzie's life because of a school project. Calvin, because contrast between main characters is a requirement for a Hutchinson novel, self-injures and has recently spiraled. <br /><br />A classmate who’s a detective, a very annoying Sherlock. <br /><br />A best friend that’s ultra accepting (of course, right?!), and a gender neutral character. <br /><br />Then Tommy, but we don’t see much of him, because Shaun decided to do that whole every so often toss in a journal entry of when the world included Tommy, thing. <br /><br />Race is thrown in there, but barely mentioned.<br /><br />WOW so many people, so little character development. <br /><br />Lua concerned me the most. A gender fluid person was a perfect opportunity to explore themes that are contentious, all too often politicized, and the overall concept is perplexing for many people. She’s a queen, and vacillates between confidence and self-doubt. His/her (pronouns were based strictly on Ozzie's examination of Lau's clothing, so we see a troubling phenomena (AGAINNNNN), where Lau's world is seen through the gaze of a non gender fluid individual. Most troubling is that we often see the world of females through the gaze of heterosexual males… so, right….) actions and conversation were almost always framed with a sort of meanness. I am all about character diversity, and not broadcasting the belief that we need YA LGBT themed novels wrapped in smiley paper, but gender neutral is so new, so misunderstood, that I believe it's harmful not to take up a more positive character. <br /><br />More concerning is the fact that a hate crime—yes this is how i took it—was tossed into the mix, but left floating, unresolved, both in discussions between her 'friends' (some fucking friends!) and legally. The main complaint, well one of them, of Thirteen Reasons Why, is that parents didn’t notice Hannah's slow descent into depression and do anything about it, but at least w/ TRW it was mostly under the radar. Here, however, the mother was aware of the cause of her daughter's injuries (hate crime or not), but I personally felt that she did not uphold a parenting role/responsibilities. <br /><br />Then we have a ‘friend’ seeing two horrible things happen to his ‘friends’. First child abuse, and second self-injury, yet in one case he did nothing, and in another he reported the CAUSE, rather than the result. So, if he didn’t figure out why one was happening he probably wouldn’t have intervened. Nice fucking friend. Oh, and his mentioning of it and the result of it being reported? that was a mistake… he actually thought nothing would happen when he broached it with someone else. <br /><br />And the ending, ugh. It was like when you read a book, with a so-so open-ended conclusion, and it might be a lame arse way of making a sequel, but then it follows up with an epilogue, but in this case that last chapter wasn’t a separate, unnecessary thing (don’t let me go!), but the actual ending. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="4691423296152427887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5309738-kaje-harper?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2311179024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15924347171724704110">4.5 stars - this was different. Ozzie is a gay teen, a high-school senior, who woke up one day to find that his boyhood best friend and now boyfriend Tommy was missing. Not only that, but all evidence of Tommy had been erased from the world - his own mother didn't remember having a son, his gifts to Ozzie had vanished, no one in school recalled his presence. Ozzie though, had a lifetime of memories of Tommy, and he's determined to hang onto them and to find Tommy and figure out what happened. He</span>
  <span id="freeText15924347171724704110" style="display:none">4.5 stars - this was different. Ozzie is a gay teen, a high-school senior, who woke up one day to find that his boyhood best friend and now boyfriend Tommy was missing. Not only that, but all evidence of Tommy had been erased from the world - his own mother didn't remember having a son, his gifts to Ozzie had vanished, no one in school recalled his presence. Ozzie though, had a lifetime of memories of Tommy, and he's determined to hang onto them and to find Tommy and figure out what happened. He has a dozen theories, from parallel universes to alien activity. His parents and his shrinks all think it's in his head. His friends probably do too, even as they try to be supportive.<br /><br />In many other ways, this senior year of school is becoming a mess. His best friend Lua, who is genderfluid, is having success with their band but not their personal life, and their friendship has been strained by the Tommy issue as well. Ozzie's parents are getting a divorce, his brother is joining the military, and he's assigned to do a physics project with a loner kid who seemed to self-destruct in the last year. <br /><br />And Ozzie seems to be the only person to notice that the universe is shrinking. Every day when he Googles "the size of the universe" the estimate up on science sites is smaller. Yet, no one else is capable of remembering differently from one day to the next. And Ozzie has to try to live his ordinary life, worked in between the sharp splinters of loss and change and weirdness and impending disaster. <br /><br />Calvin, his physics partner, turns out to be a smart, troubled gay boy with secrets of his own. And as they grow closer together, he's the only one who seems to really believe that Ozzie isn't just crazy, that Tommy might actually exist somewhere, and that something weird is going on. But Calvin has his own demons, and not much time or energy to deal with Ozzie's. <br /><br />The story pulled me in and kept me fascinated. In its very improbability, it still had a lot to say about being human and growing up. The ending was satisfying, if not the one I wanted. This author's talent for on-the-edge-of-reality stories is well worth the reading time.<br /><br />Content warning for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ self-harm, domestic violence off page, physical and sexual abuse history <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

		<td>
		    <b>
          <a title="What&#39;s the Name of That Book???" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21423222-solved-a-ya-book-about-a-boy-that-is-gay-and-has-a-crush-on-his-classma">What&#39;s the Name o...:</a>
        </b>
      
			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/21423222-solved-a-ya-book-about-a-boy-that-is-gay-and-has-a-crush-on-his-classma">SOLVED. A YA book about a boy that is gay and has a crush on his classmate, spoilers ahead. [s]</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0BN0AcB+A8Q/nAMdImsp1XfZDxoxlisC3VcUI895Qc3WnemlwtHtypUK+aDe487Y4UqeZQ5hgLO9NzpyT/FPeg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('e6gYDsJej9kg7xzRz4owIAyH1yVnQTpO1Z0YyauSnwt9JoWqwPFh14p55mwzAtctmhRGWli2kf+1/TaYKxqRvA==')})
  }
